- Can the fine wine market maintain its performance? | FT Wealth: Kind: captions Language: en-GB Few would argue that we aren't living in turbulent times. But today I've come to a particularly serene part of southeast England to find out how an evolving fine wine market is responding to an assortment of traditional and not so traditional challenges, ranging from long-term weather patterns to pandemics. It's very strong. The market has moved from being a UK-centric type investment to right now, worldwide. And there are more platforms cycling up all the time. In fact, the Live-ex 100, the leading industry exchange of the secondary market, has risen by around a third over the past two years. They are very, very good at putting out information as to the state of the wine market in general. And they are saying that it's extremely robust. Over the past 10 years, when it comes to luxury investments' performance, wine has trailed only cars and rare whisky, according to the Knight Frank Investment Index. But this global market is facing a global issue that could well affect investment values and prices in the longer term - climate change. The major wine regions for investment are all on the same latitude. So you start off with Napa Valley in California, on the West Coast of the United States, through to Bordeaux, through to Burgundy. And of course, these regions are getting hotter and/or colder. As our climate changes, it could make it harder to produce future vintages of the quality we're used to. Climate change is going to play a part in the wine life. That said, with wine, if there is no more Bordeaux, then the wines that are held with customers are only going to increase in value. Of course, climate change could eventually influence supply the other way by actually opening up new areas for quality wine production. The Champagne region of France, for example, is an area seen as being particularly susceptible to climate change, which is why famed champagne houses, like Pomeroy and Taittinger, are now looking to places they would never previously have considered for production. Big international players have come into the English wine scene to invest in their own vineyards, put their money where their mouth is, and recognise how special the southeast of England is. The effect of climate change will, of course, be gradual. But I have come to the UK's oldest wine merchant, Berry Brothers, founded in 1698, to learn more about how certain aspects of the markets are changing now. Some good ordinary clarets, are they? Yes, my favourite. The company stores around £1bn worth of wine for private clients. There may be a few thousand bottles here, maybe. I actually don't know the exact figure. In Basingstoke we currently have a facility for 9mn bottles. Spending on fine wines actually increased throughout the recent pandemic. And sales for its en Primeur season, which allows collectors to buy the most recent vintage before it is bottled, were up over 40 per cent in 2021 compared with 2019. As sales grow, and with greater volumes being traded across the world, ensuring the origin and care of their wine is as it should be is becoming ever more important for collectors. The bulk of what I do is looking to check that everything that we sell is absolutely as pristine as it should be, and that it's come from the right place so that the provenance is correct, that it's been stored correctly on the way into us. In times of higher inflation, as we're seeing now, physical assets do have the potential to offer solid returns. But wine can entail higher costs than other investments due to factors like shipping and storage. It's seldom a short-term investment, and concerns about counterfeiting are also becoming more prominent. It wasn't really an issue up until about 10 years ago. Now it's something which everyone asks us about. Cases like that of Rudy Kurniawan, who in 2013 was convicted of wine fraud for selling tens of millions of fake wines, have thrown a spotlight on counterfeiting. So how big of a problem is wine fraud nowadays? The more common types of wine fraud is mass produced bottling of perfectly potable table wine with a more expensive label on it. And that's happening probably as we speak. Somewhere in the world, somebody is bottling something which is not what it says on the label. Combating the frauds takes various techniques and technologies, both old and more recent. Can you see the fluorescing lights and the fluorescing label there? Yeah. But there are two tiny little holograms in the shape of a cloverleaf. Their position moves with every vintage. Other verification systems can range from nanochips to tracking numbers and even a system that matches a unique pattern of air bubbles under the cap to an image kept on file. One relatively recent development uses blockchain technology to help gain clarity over a bottle's condition and whereabouts across the supply chain. When you've got a broken supply chain, actually, that's the best time, in my opinion, to commit fraud. So what we're doing is saying, actually, we may make 5,000 or 500, depending on what's going out of a QR code that will be unique to that bottle and that batch. Can you show me how this sort of technology would actually work in practise then? We're going to scan that code as so, and now we're going to go into temperature, humidity, and GPS location of that bottle. Systems like Chainvine won't be for everyone. But in a global survey of wineries last year, blockchain technology ranked second of all technologies they saw as having the highest priority of importance to develop. As for the wine investment market, if it wants to build on strong recent performance, it will have to adapt to the constant changes coming from both inside and outside the industry.

- How to start a wine cellar with Rudy von Strasser: Kind: captions Language: en hi friends rudy von strasser here in my wine cellar and i'd like to take a moment to talk to you about aging wines and what a better place to do it first of all it's important to know that as soon as a wine is put into the bottle aging starts and that aging is a series of chemical reactions now in a cold cellar and the reason people keep sellers cool is because those reactions happen slower and in a more controlled situation in a warm environment like on your kitchen counter where it's 85 degrees in texas those changes are going to go a lot faster so if you'd like to age wine if you plan on aging wine it's important to have temperature control also you need to know that bottle size matters if you look at this six liter bottle it's going to age much slower than a magnum or a 750 or even a little 375. so if you have 375s at home drink them sooner than you would drink a bigger bottle i want to talk about what happens in aging and i've drawn a little graph that i want to show you and this graph on the x-axis has time and on the y-axis has concentration and the red line i drew is what i call fruit characteristics and in a white wine fruit characteristics maybe um like in a riesling or an esophageal blanc be melons and peaches and apricots aromas like that in a red wine it may be strawberries brambles those aromas start out very high when a wine is very young over time those aromas start to go away and as the wine gets 15 20 years old there won't be any fruit aromas left or very very subtle fruit aromas the blue line i drew i marked as aged characteristics those start out at zero when a wine is young and over time they'll start to increase and the important thing with this graph is that at any time you drink this wine it's going to have very different characteristics as a young wine it's going to be mainly fruit and very little age characteristics at four or five six years old it'll have some fruit and it'll have some age characteristics um you know in the 10-year period it might have a little bit of both but the fruit is starting to go away and at 15 20 30 years - Wine Guardian - How To Build a Wine Cellar: Kind: captions Language: en Are you thinking about building a wine cellar, but aren’t sure where to start? Let us do the heavy lifting for you! This informational video will show you how to build the perfect cellar from start to finish. Your fine wine collection needs proper storage for long-term aging. Location plays a big part in planning your wine storage space. Choosing a location that is naturally cool, free of vibration, and away from natural UV light sources is essential. Natural UV light can impact the stability of wine and create an unpleasant taste and smell. Vibration from mechanical equipment such as a furnace or laundry equipment can also impact the stability of wine causing it to lose its quality, aroma and flavor. In addition to these general guidelines, knowing which type of wine cellar cooling unit you need to use is beneficial before starting construction. When planning a wine room, you should place the wine cooling equipment relatively close to the wine storage area. Framing your wine cellar will help outline your space and support the insulation, vapor barrier, and remainder of the wall system including sheetrock and/or stone surfaces. In the US, 2x4 and 2x6 stud sizes are common components for construction. Outside the US, common materials may include block or stone walls. Humidity is a critical, yet often overlooked element in wine cellar design. Without ideal humidity, wine quality may be affected. 50% to 70% relative humidity is adequate, while 60% is ideal. Humidity higher than 70% will likely cause mold and degradation of the labels and glue. Humidity below 50% will dry out corks resulting in evaporation and degradation of the wine. Vapor barriers help maintain a proper humidity range. Closed-cell spray foam is a common insulation method and it provides excellent vapor barrier and insulative properties. A more traditional construction method is using a minimum of 6 mm plastic sheeting as a vapor barrier around the outside, or warm side, of the studs and ceiling structure above. If there is no access to the outside of the stud wall structure, wrap the interior while leaving the plastic in the stud cavity loose so installation can be applied between the studs. Another solution is to leave a 1-inch air gap between any foundation wall and new stud wall, and to apply the plastic to the outside of the new stud wall as it’s being built. We recommend using dry lock or another sealant to seal the floors and any exposed concrete walls. If you are using glass in your wine room, we strongly encourage using insulated glass. Doors to the wine room should be exterior-grade with a proper threshold and seals on all four sides to keep air leakage to a minimum. A poorly sealed door can create air leakage, causing the cooling unit to run continuously. It can also affect temperature and humidity control and potentially damage the cooling unit. When it comes to insulation, a minimum of r-13, or 10cm depth-insulation, should be applied to the interior cellar walls while exterior walls may require r-19, or 14cm-depth insulation, depending on wall thickness. R-21, or 15cm depth-insulation, can be used for the ceiling and non-ground level floors, but we recommend r-30, or 23.5cm-depth insulation, or higher. Flooring should be able to withstand humid conditions. Avoid using carpet and use tile or hardwood floors instead. The two most common types of wine racking are traditional wood racking and modern metal racking. Feel free to pick wine racking based on personal preference. Wine Guardian offers five types of wine cellar cooling units: ducted, ducted split, ductless split, through-the-wall and ceiling mount. Each of these cooling units has its own installation requirements, and its use depends on the size of the wine room, the ability to run ductwork, the mounting location and venting capabilities. Wine Guardian also offers either integrated or standalone wine-cellar humidifiers, which add moisture to any wine cellar. Integrated humidifiers attach to Wine Guardian cooling units while Wine Guardian standalone humidifiers can operate independently. A heat load calculator, such as our wine cellar cooling calculator, should be used to determine your wine room’s required cooling capacity and which size unit you may need. Insulation values, room size, type of use (commercial vs residential), and construction materials such as glass, block, sheet rock, or natural stone walls will impact the capacity needed to condition the wine cellar. Once you determine your cooling capacity, you’ll have to choose the type of unit that is suitable for your wine room. Be sure to choose a system that is appropriate for the space you have to mount the mechanical equipment, keeping in mind electrical, drainage, and venting requirements. If you have a cooling system that requires ductwork, make sure to use insulated ductwork for both the supply and return duct runs. We recommend minimal-length duct runs to maximize cooling unit efficiency and temperature stability. In the wine cellar, we recommend placing the incoming air supply in a high location in or near the ceiling, while placing the return duct near the floor to minimize temperature gradients in the space. Depending on the size of the room, you may require multiple supply and return locations to provide sufficient distribution of air to all areas of the wine room. It is important to make sure the cooling unit is properly installed and mounted. The unit should not be placed in a confined space where it cannot receive sufficient airflow or properly exhaust heat. Self-contained units, such as ducted or through-the-wall units, should be vented to a space three times the size of the wine room. Ducted split and ductless split cooling units exhaust heat through the condensing unit which is typically mounted outside. Wine Guardian cooling systems require a dedicated power supply, control wiring for proper operation, and drain piping to an open floor drain, sink, or condensate pump. To avoid water leaks and damage to racking and floors, it is important to make sure the cooling unit is properly piped to a drain and placed on an elevated stand. Each Wine Guardian unit has its own drain connection requirements which should be followed for successful unit installation. Humidifiers require a water-supply line and a drain line for proper operation. To avoid degradation of your wine, it is important to control your cellar’s environment based on the air temperature and not on the liquid temperature of your wine. Generally, the thermostat should be placed at the midpoint of the room and in an area that has sufficient airflow. You can place up to three remote sensors, which are exclusive to Wine Guardian, in the wine room (at top, bottom, and midpoint) to gain more accurate control of the space. Air temperature changes faster than wine temperature, so regulating air temperature will create a more stable environment for your wine. In addition to being unresponsive, bottle probes force the wine to change temperature (something you don’t want), are unable to monitor humidity levels, and have the tendency for the cooling unit to run longer. Proper long-term wine storage requires a temperature between 55 and 58°F (13 and 14°C). Temperatures outside the recommended temperature range will accelerate or decelerate the aging process of your fine wine collection. For commercial applications such as restaurants, you may want to store some bottles of wine at consumption temperature. Our serving temperature cooling option allows you to serve a bottle at the perfect temperature directly from the cabinet or cellar. Wine Guardian units configured for serving temperature cooling provide an extended temperature cooling range from 42-64°F (5-18°C). Before turning on the wine cooling system, make sure all construction is finished, openings are sealed, there are no gaps in glass panels, and racking has been installed. Once the cooling unit is installed and operational, it may take several hours to bring the room to wine storage conditions, which is normal. Once your wine room is at wine storage temperature, you can load your wine into the space, but keep in mind that the unit may run continuously while your wine gets down to temperature. Once your wine is cooled to set point, normal run times should be about 15-20 minutes per hour. For additional assistance, contact one of our wine cellar experts or use our distributor page to find a distributor near you.

- How to Build a Wine Wall | Wine Guardian: Kind: captions Language: en Interested in building a wine wall but not sure&nbsp; where to start? Wine Guardian is here to help.&nbsp;&nbsp; With decades of experience creating the perfect&nbsp; environments for preserving fine wine, we know&nbsp;&nbsp; what it takes to turn your vision of a stylish&nbsp; and functional wine wall into a reality. Join&nbsp;&nbsp; us as we walk through the five steps of wine wall&nbsp; construction and learn some important tips that&nbsp;&nbsp; can help ensure the success of your project. Many&nbsp; external factors can affect the stability of your&nbsp;&nbsp; wine collection. That's why choosing the right&nbsp; location for your wine wall is such a critical&nbsp;&nbsp; part of the planning process. It's important&nbsp; to find a location away from natural UV light&nbsp;&nbsp; sources like external windows that can harm your&nbsp; wine collection leading to unpleasant tastes or&nbsp;&nbsp; smells. You also want to make sure your wine wall&nbsp; won't be subjected to vibrations from sources like&nbsp;&nbsp; household appliances or foot traffic. Studies have&nbsp; shown that even slight constant vibrations can&nbsp;&nbsp; impact the flavor of your wine within 18 months&nbsp; or less. In addition to these general guidelines&nbsp;&nbsp; for selecting your wine wall location we also&nbsp; recommend considering the type of cooling unit&nbsp;&nbsp; you plan on using during this first step. By doing&nbsp; so you can properly allocate space to ensure your&nbsp;&nbsp; cooling equipment is close to the wine wall so you&nbsp; can minimize its impact on the aesthetics of your project. Once you've chosen the right location for&nbsp; your wine wall it's time to plan its construction.&nbsp;&nbsp; Temperature and humidity control, air leakage, and&nbsp; how you plan on showcasing your collection are all&nbsp;&nbsp; things to keep in mind as you begin this process.&nbsp; You should start by deciding how much glass you&nbsp;&nbsp; will need for your project. Will your wine&nbsp; wall have glass on all four sides or just one?&nbsp;&nbsp; Either way we recommend using insulated glass to&nbsp; prevent heat conduction and making sure that any&nbsp;&nbsp; seams between glass windows or doors are properly&nbsp; sealed. Any walls that are not glass can be framed&nbsp;&nbsp; with 2x4 or 2x6 studs. Other common framing&nbsp; materials may include block or stone walls.&nbsp;&nbsp; Framing the space will help outline your wine wall&nbsp; and support your insulation and vapor barrier as&nbsp;&nbsp; well as the remainder of the wall system whether&nbsp; it's sheetrock, stone, or a combination. When it&nbsp;&nbsp; comes to insulation we recommend using a minimum&nbsp; of R13 or 9cm depth insulation for interior walls,&nbsp;&nbsp; R19 or 14cm depth insulation for exterior walls,&nbsp; and R30 or 23.5cm depth insulation or higher for&nbsp;&nbsp; ceiling and non- ground level floors. Creating a&nbsp; vapor barrier helps to maintain proper humidity in&nbsp;&nbsp; the wine wall. In addition to providing excellent&nbsp; insulation closed cell spray foam can also act&nbsp;&nbsp; as a vapor barrier. Alternatively you can use 6mm&nbsp; plastic sheeting as a vapor barrier by applying it&nbsp;&nbsp; around the outside or warm side of the studs and&nbsp; ceiling structure. For the flooring we recommend&nbsp;&nbsp; using either tile or hardwood. Not only do these&nbsp; materials withstand elevated humidity levels they&nbsp;&nbsp; also have a timeless look that can add uniqueness&nbsp; to your space. It's also important to consider&nbsp;&nbsp; the number of doors there are to your wine wall&nbsp; and how often they will be open. Due to their&nbsp;&nbsp; size each opening replaces a large percentage&nbsp; of the room's air. To reduce their impact make&nbsp;&nbsp; sure all doors to your wine wall are exterior&nbsp; grade with a proper threshold and seals on all&nbsp;&nbsp; four sides to minimize air leakage. Even a small&nbsp; leak can affect temperature and humidity and cause&nbsp;&nbsp; your cooling unit to run continuously shortening&nbsp; its lifespan and increasing energy costs. Lastly&nbsp;&nbsp; you'll want to determine both the size and type&nbsp; of racking system that would work best for you.&nbsp;&nbsp; A label forward racking system makes it easy for&nbsp; you and your guests to admire each bottle of wine.&nbsp;&nbsp; Other options include cork forward or angled&nbsp; racking or even a combination of the two. The&nbsp;&nbsp; key is choosing a design that makes sense for&nbsp; your space and matches your unique taste and style. By using the Wine Guardian Wine Cellar&nbsp; Cooling Calculator you can get equipment&nbsp;&nbsp; recommendations based on variables like wine&nbsp; wall size, insulation type, usage frequency,&nbsp;&nbsp; and more. The calculator will recommend a range&nbsp; of options with the appropriate cooling capacity&nbsp;&nbsp; for your wine wall. From there you'll be able&nbsp; to decide which system is appropriate based&nbsp;&nbsp; on the space you have for mounting the&nbsp; mechanical equipment. It's important to&nbsp;&nbsp; keep in mind your system's electrical,&nbsp; drainage, and venting requirements and&nbsp;&nbsp; whether you plan on using the wine wall&nbsp; strictly for long-term storage or if you&nbsp;&nbsp; will need serving temperature capabilities as&nbsp; well. Whatever you have in mind we'll help you&nbsp;&nbsp; find the right equipment to provide optimal&nbsp; storage conditions for your wine collection. Installing and mounting your cooling&nbsp; unit correctly is essential for long-term&nbsp;&nbsp; performance and efficiency. It all starts with&nbsp; positioning your unit in an unconfined location&nbsp;&nbsp; that allows for sufficient air flow. Depending on&nbsp; the type of cooling system and mounting location&nbsp;&nbsp; you'll need connections for primary power,&nbsp; duct work, control wiring, drain piping,&nbsp;&nbsp; and for split systems, refrigerant piping. The&nbsp; system's thermostat should be placed midpoint on&nbsp;&nbsp; a wall in an area with good air flow to ensure&nbsp; accurate temperature readings. Because of the&nbsp;&nbsp; large amount of glass in wine walls placing the&nbsp; thermostat in an optimal location can sometimes&nbsp;&nbsp; be difficult. That's why up to three Wine Guardian&nbsp; designed remote sensors can be placed at the top,&nbsp;&nbsp; bottom, midpoint, or at the return grill of your&nbsp; wine wall to maximize system accuracy. When these&nbsp;&nbsp; remote sensors are used the thermostat can even&nbsp; be placed outside the wine wall for convenient&nbsp;&nbsp; access. Wine Guardian Wine Wall Cooling Systems&nbsp; mount completely flush with the ceiling in a wine&nbsp;&nbsp; wall. For these and other ceiling mounted units&nbsp; it's important to make sure your supply air is&nbsp;&nbsp; not facing any glass to prevent condensation.&nbsp; You'll also want to make sure the system is&nbsp;&nbsp; installed in a location that can be accessed&nbsp; easily for maintenance. For ducted and ducted&nbsp;&nbsp; split system installations following several best&nbsp; practices can help you maximize cooling efficiency&nbsp;&nbsp; and temperature stability. This includes using&nbsp; insulated duct work for supply and return runs,&nbsp;&nbsp; minimizing the length of duct runs where possible,&nbsp; placing incoming air supply ducts in an elevated&nbsp;&nbsp; position in or near the ceiling, and placing&nbsp; return ducts near the floor. Depending on the&nbsp;&nbsp; size of the wine wall you may require multiple&nbsp; supply and return locations to provide sufficient&nbsp;&nbsp; distribution of air to all areas of the wine&nbsp; room. To maintain proper humidity in the wine&nbsp;&nbsp; wall ducted and ducted split systems can be&nbsp; equipped with a humidifier mounted directly to&nbsp;&nbsp; the system itself. Lastly, self-contained ducted&nbsp; systems should be vented to a space three times&nbsp;&nbsp; the size of the wine room whereas split systems&nbsp; exhaust heat through an exterior condensing unit. Once the installation of your equipment is&nbsp; complete it's time to turn on your cooling system.&nbsp;&nbsp; Before you flip the switch make sure to do a final&nbsp; check on the following items: confirm all openings&nbsp;&nbsp; are sealed, that there are no gaps in the glass&nbsp; panels, and that your racking system is installed&nbsp;&nbsp; properly. If everything looks good you're ready&nbsp; to turn on your cooling unit. Depending on the&nbsp;&nbsp; size of your wine wall it may take a few hours for&nbsp; it to reach optimal wine storage conditions. Once&nbsp;&nbsp; the correct temperature has been reached you&nbsp; can begin loading your wine. Don't be alarmed&nbsp;&nbsp; if the unit initially runs continuously. You&nbsp; may need to increase the temperature set set&nbsp;&nbsp; point and then step down the temperature in&nbsp; 2°F or 1°C increments until the desired set&nbsp;&nbsp; point is reached and the system cycles on and off.&nbsp; Once conditions level out your work is complete.&nbsp;&nbsp; Congratulations on finishing your wine wall! Feel&nbsp; free to contact one of our wine wall experts for&nbsp;&nbsp; additional assistance or use the distributor&nbsp; page on our website to find a distributor near you.

- Getting Into Wine: A Beginner's Crash Course: Kind: captions Language: en Have you ever stared at a wall of wine and had no idea where to start? In the next 20 minutes or so, we're gonna fix that. Hi, my name is Madeline Puckette and I've spent 15 years helping people learn about wine. And if you need some proof, check out Wine Folly's online resource I co-created or my books, which have been printed in 27 languages. The main reason people find it difficult to learn wine is because it's hard to get started. It seems really cool, but it's also overwhelming. How do you choose what you like? Unless you already know a lot, you're essentially rolling the dice with each wine purchase. My goal for you in this video is to give you enough of a foundation so you know how to ask the right questions to drink better wine. After this, choosing a wine shouldn't fill you with low-key dread. Instead, it should feel more like a fun adventure. In short, I want to show you all the things I wish I knew when I was getting started. Being confident in wine helps unlock doors in social situations and it can also help you get a better-paying job. So if you want to make your Wine 101 official, check out Wine Folly's certification courses. Wine is just fermented grape juice, right? This basic concept holds answers that most people overlook and knowing them can help you drink better wine. To answer your question, yes, wine is fermented grapes. Sure, there are wines made with other fruit too, like cranberry wine or pineapple wine, but if it's labeled just wine, that means it's made with just grapes. Of course, the grapes used for making wine look different from table grapes. They're smaller, they're sweeter, they have thicker skins and seeds. These differences give wine its unique taste. Wine grapes also behave differently than other fruit. They don't continue to ripen after they've been picked. And this means wine making happens once a year, right after harvest. And that's what the vintage date means on a wine label. It's the year the grapes were harvested. The vintage is actually where I made my first wine mistake. This was around 2007. I know, it was a long time ago. I bought a bottle of 2005 Côte du Rhône, back when you could get a pretty great red wine for just $13. Côte du Rhône, in case you don't know, this isn't a single-varietal wine like Cabernet Sauvignon. This was a red wine made with three different grapes, Grenache, Syrah and Mourvèdre. And the name Côte du Rhône is the name of the region where the red wine is from. At the time, this was my first Côte du Rhône, and I loved it so much that I ordered a bunch more. The wine arrived from the same region and the same producer, but it wasn't the same vintage. It was the 2006 and not the 2005. And it didn't taste the same at all. I was devastated. Turns out, the weather in each vintage affects how the grapes ripen, which in turn changes the quality of the wine. 2005 was quite warm and dry and produced rich, fruit-forward red wines, which I loved. Yum, yum, yum. 2006, on the other hand, was a little cooler and the critics would call it "fresher," meaning lighter bodied and more tart. The 2006 has ended up aging quite well, but that didn't give me that yum, yum, yum taste I was looking for. We call this vintage variation. It's one of wine's great strengths and great weaknesses. The lesson for me was if I was looking for French red wines like Côte du Rhône, I tended to prefer, at least back then, wines from warmer, drier years. So when you find a wine you like, pay attention to the weather from that vintage. It's a clue. But the thing that most affects how a wine tastes is where it comes from. Over the years, I've mapped thousands of different wine regions around the world. But if you're just getting started, don't try to memorize them. Instead, let's categorize the regions by climate. To do that, I want to go back to where it all started at least 8,000 years ago in the mountainous region of Caucasus. This is where we think winemaking first started. I like to think that the first wine was a complete accident. We had some extra grapes, we put them in a container, forgot about them for a little while, and came back and yuck! Then someone was bold enough to drink it, and this is what started our multi-millenia obsession with wine. Winemaking expanded with Western civilization into the Mediterranean basin. Vines adapted over thousands of years to different climates, cooler climates in the north and hotter climates in the south. And this is why there are thousands of different grape varieties in several different shades. What's surprising is that everything from lush Chardonnay to dry-as-a-bone Sangiovese are all cultivars of the same species. Vitis vinifera, aka the wine grape. And the climate where Vitis vinifera grows is where we get the concept of the wine belt, which includes the latitudes of around 30 degrees north to 50 north and about 30 to 45 south. There are exceptions to the rule, but for the most part, this is where wine grows. And here's the kicker and the big aha. Vines that grow in warmer climates like Shiraz in Barossa, Australia, or Cabernet Sauvignon in Paso Robles, California, tend to produce riper, sweeter grapes, which in turn produces bolder, more fruit-forward wines. In fact, warm climates produce some of the world's best bold red wines. And contrasting that, when you go north, like to Champagne in France or to the Willamette Valley in Oregon, the grapes there produce less sugar content, which results in lighter-bodied, more tart-tasting wines. And guess what? Cool climate regions produce some of the world's best elegant white and red wines. So knowing about warm climate wines and cool climate wines is an absolute game changer. It explains why two wines made from the same grape can taste completely different, and it's a key to consistently finding wine styles that you love. Now, you might be thinking, "This is great, Madeline, but what about all the different grapes? We've got Merlot, Pinot Noir, Chardonnay. It's a lot to remember." And honestly, you're right. With over a thousand different commercially viable wine varieties out there, I wouldn't worry too much about memorizing all of them, save for the ones you love. Instead, we can simplify things again, taking into consideration all the wines of the world, and group them into more or less nine different wine styles. Sparkling wines, light-bodied white wines, aromatic white wines, full-bodied white wines, rosé and orange wine, light-bodied reds, medium-bodied red wines, full-bodied red wines, and dessert and fortified wines. Now, this categorization model covers most of the wine that's out there in the market, so knowing about each of these styles will help you communicate what you want. I'm going to give you an example of what to look for in each category, a great value as well as a benchmark examples. But remember, this is just the tip of the iceberg. Let's start with full-bodied red wines. These are your whole milk, heavy cream of the wine world. However, unlike milk, they don't get their big body from fat content. They get it from alcohol and tannin. What is tannin? It's a plant polyphenol. Sidebar, tannins contains nearly all of wine's health benefits, and texturally, it gives wines layers of complex velvety flavor. Too much tannin in wines taste bitter and astringent, but when it's just right, it's magical. Cabernet Sauvignon is your benchmark bold red wine. In fact, this is the most widely planted wine grape in the world. But if you're looking for value in the U.S., try Petite Sirah. And if you're elsewhere in the world, try Alicante Bouschet from Portugal. These alts aren't as well known about and thus offer great value. Medium-bodied red wines. Have you ever heard someone say, "Oh, that's a food wine." This is what medium-bodied red wines are all about. Instead of being dominated by brooding blueberry and blackberry flavors like a bold red, they tend to have more red fruit notes, like raspberry, cherry, and plum. They also have fresher acidity, which makes them better to pair with food. There are many varieties that fit this category, but the one you should absolutely know about is Côtes du Rhône. This is a regional wine from southern France that blends with Grenache, with Syrah, and Mourvèdre. These three varieties actually grow in regions all around the world in warmer climates. So if you see this blend, snatch it up and give it a try. For value, try this wine from Italy called Montepulciano d'Abruzzo. I consistently cannot believe the quality I find in this wine for the price. Light-bodied red wines. For those of you who hate the bitterness of tannin but love red wine, this is your go-to wine style, and Pinot Noir is your benchmark grape. This wine is so light and delicate you can see your hand through it. They serve it in a big globe-shaped wine glass to capture the airy red fruits and rose aromas that Pinot Noir is all about. Pinot Noir is great, but it's also one of the most expensive red wines out there. So for value, maybe try a bottle of Frappato from Sicily. It has everything we love about light-bodied red wines, but because it's so lesser known about, it tends to be less expensive. So that's red wines. Basically, it's a spectrum from light to bold, and it correlates with the grape variety. And since most red wines are dry, as in not sweet, the fruit factor you may taste on some is more closely associated with the climate where the grape is grown. Cooler climates tend to produce more tart, sour fruit flavors, and warmer climates make wines with riper, more baked fruit notes. Now for the rest of the wine styles. Sparkling wine. It comes in both dry and sweet styles from Brut to Demi-Sec. I like to think of sparkling wine as kind of the most technical winemaker's wine because to make it, it takes two fermentations. One for the alcohol and two for the bubbles. The benchmark is definitely champagne. Champagne is what you would call a regional wine, meaning it can only be made in the region of Champagne in France. And the reason why I say champagne is the benchmark is because they're the ones that perfected a sparkling winemaking method that's been emulated all around the world. But if you're looking for value, try Cava. This is also a regional wine made in Spain, and it uses the same method as champagne, but with Spanish grapes. And also because it's less known about and less popular, it tends to offer better value. Light-bodied white wines. These zippy, dry white wines have citrus and apple notes. And the thing that defines this category isn't the grape variety necessarily, it's that these wines rarely age in oak barrels. Turns out, stainless steel winemakings helps preserve the crisp quality in white wines. Quality for dollar, light-bodied white wines are some of the best wine values out there. Pinot Grigio might just be your benchmark light-bodied white wine. In France, they call it Pinot Gris, and it grows around the world. Looking for a great value? Try Picpoul de Pinet, a regional wine from France. It absolutely sings for the price. Full-bodied white wines. These rich, toasty, dry white wines have lots of body and a creamy texture. Chardonnay is our benchmark. Surprisingly, it's not the Chardonnay grape that gives these wines a creamy, buttery texture. It's the winemaking interaction that happens when Chardonnay ages in oak barrels. In fact, every white wine you find aged in barrels tends to have richer, more full-bodied flavors. If you're looking for value, try Grenache Blanc aged in oak, for an amazing alternative with a very unique taste. Aromatic white wines. There are some white wine varieties out there that are so aromatic that they deserve their own category. They range in taste from bone-dried to sweet, but can easily be mistaken for a sweet wine because of their candied nose of orange blossom and peach. Riesling is your benchmark. And Germany is its home base, making some of the most haunting Riesling in the world. Of course, on the hunt for value, check out Torrontés from Argentina. This wine is typically made dry, but it smells sweet, like potpourri. Rosé and orange wine. Rosé isn't a grape. It's more of a winemaking process using red grapes. Rosé happens when you stop the grape skins from dyeing the wine red by taking them out of the fermentation, midway. And this means Rosé can be made with nearly any red wine grape. So try your favorite red wine as a Rosé, and you'll be surprised by the totally different expression you get from your favorite grape. So what about orange wine? Just like Rosé is a winemaking method using red grapes, orange wine is a method using white wine grapes. Orange wine happens when you ferment a white wine the way you would a red. By leaving the juice fermenting with the skins, the technique is rare, but it makes some of the richest, most red wine-like white wines I have ever tasted. Dessert and fortified wine. Dessert wines range in style from actually pretty dry to very sweet, and sometimes they're stabilized by adding brandy in a process called fortification. Your benchmark is Port wine from Portugal. The longer that some Port ages, the more tawny in color it becomes and nuttier the taste. I once tasted a tawny that was 250 years old and I couldn't believe how good it tasted, especially since most wines don't age past 10 years. For great value, try Sherry from southern Spain. Many Sherry wines are totally dry, salty, and a little bit nutty. It's a totally unique flavor in a wine that you must try at least once in your life. Now that you know the nine common wine styles, you can better communicate and find what sounds good to you. But how do you go about looking the part when you're enjoying a glass of wine? Well, there's some things that sommeliers know that will greatly improve your wine tasting experience. This is a bottle of wine, the world's best invention. And this is maybe the world's second best invention, a waiter's friend. Knowing how to use one of these will make you feel like a real sommelier. Now we have a wine glass. There are many different styles of wine glasses, but a great wine glass has enough space above the wine to collect aromas. You only need 5 ounces or 150 milliliters for a full serving. Now, my glass might look mostly empty, but that's actually the right amount of wine because when I swirl, it releases the aromas into the bowl of glass. When it comes to tasting, I have a whole 10-minute video on the secrets to tasting like a pro, but conceptually wine tasting is much simpler than that. Smell the wine before you taste it. If you block your nose, you won't pick up any of those aromas of black currant, cedar, and peppercorn on a fine Bordeaux. And that's really the secret. It's really that simple. Smell before you taste. If you do this with every new wine you try, you're going to learn so, so much. So let's bring it all together. We started this journey at a massive wall of wine, feeling a little lost. But now you have a powerful new framework. You know that vintage tells you about the weather of a single year and why the same wine can taste different from one year to the next. You've learned that climate is your secret weapon. By knowing whether a wine comes from a warm or cool region, you can predict if the wine will be bold and fruity or light and elegant. And instead of getting bogged down by thousands of grape varieties, you can now confidently navigate the world of wine using the nine core styles. Finally, you've learned that tasting wine isn't actually about snobbery, it's simply about paying attention. The act of smelling your wine before you sip it is the single biggest thing that will elevate your experience and unblock all those amazing flavors that we've been talking about. My promise to you at the beginning of this video was to help you see wine differently. So the next time you walk into a wine shop, that wall of wine won't be so intimidating. Instead of seeing endless confusing options, you see possibilities. You can now ask the right questions. "Hey, I'm in the mood for something from a cool climate." Or "Hey, I really like full bodied red wines, what's a great value alternative to Cabernet Sauvignon?" That feeling? That's confidence. And that's how you start to drink a better wine. Now, if you're excited about what you've learned and want to make your Wine Smarts official, I'd love for you to check out our Wine Folly's certification courses at winefolly.com We have everything from fun, fast beginner courses to deep dives that will get you ready for a sommelier's career. And if you want to continue this journey and learn more about wine with me, make sure you subscribe to this channel because we've got more ground to cover. Thanks for watching and I'll see you next time. Cheers.

- 6 Tips for Starting a Wine Collection | Wine Guardian: Kind: captions Language: en six tips for starting a wine collection wine collections are a commitment that will take years before yielding Rewards but if you're committed to taking your game to the next level you'll want to think it through plan and make sure you do it right here's how tip 1 Research wines always do your research before starting a wine collection especially if you don't know much about wine you should at least know the basics some wine history the classic regions and grapes and which vintages are regarded as the greatest tip 2 save money before collecting exceptional wines are rare and therefore expensive a serious wine collection requires substantial investment both in money and time tip 3 invest in wine storage at home as you build your collection a professional long-term solution may be needed sellers are superior for aging luxury wines but cost significantly higher because they require more planning more materials and Custom Construction tip 4 organize wine documentation organized receipts inventory records and routine appraisals are necessary to ensure that each wine in your Cellar is counted still drinkable and has been valued at its most current market price tip 5 avoid handling wines too frequently expensive wines should be kept as stable as possible to ensure Optimum aging reduce the risk of being dropped exposure to dramatic temperature changes and maybe less obvious soiling or ripping of the labels tip 6 acquire wine one bottle at a time be thoughtful about your purchases and ensure that you're buying what you enjoy and for the best price for more tips on wine storage visit wineguardian.com

You know, unlike retail stores where buying and selling is the role of a particular person, in a restaurant that's rarely the case. The person buying wine has a lot of other stuff to do. Scheduling wait staff and bartender staff, taking inventory, there's just a million things I gotta do. Buying wine is only a part of it. So, one of the keys secrets if you were selling to restaurants is to place a very high value on their time. So, without further ado, a couple of keys here about the secret to selling on premise. Number one, (Ben laughs) this may come as a shock to you but it's not about your product or even about you and how much you know, it's just not. I got news for you. There's a thousand Chardonnays just as good as yours at the same price. Your products are not so distinct that some restaurant has to have them. And the problem here is if you go on and on and on about your OIC regimen and how often you stirred the lees and the soil types, all that stuff is very interesting and titillating, but when it comes to selling wine in a restaurant, it just doesn't make any difference. And you are wasting your time and the buyer's time by thinking that it's somehow significant. You know, I have three lists. One is, what it's definitely not about when it comes to selling wine and spirits to restaurants. A list of what it might be about, and then a list of what it's absolutely about. And if you operate inside of that paradigm, you're gonna be so much more successful. But the bottom line is you get your focus off yourself and onto your customer. There's three things restaurants want all over the world. They want the same three things. They wanna grow their revenue, they wanna control their costs, not necessarily lower them that's a beautiful thing, but controlling them is the main thing. And then they wanna improve guest satisfaction. How is your product gonna help them do that? If you don't understand what the components are for each of those three things, you're not gonna be very successful. You'll be like everyone else, comes in with their sample bag full of stuff that you need to sell. By the way, when do you do that, where's your focus? Is your focus on the customer? Absolutely not. It's on you and what you need to sell. And it's just disgusting, it gives all sales, professional salespeople, those of us who consider ourselves to be professional salespeople. It gives us a bad name. See, I have sold millions of cases of wine to restaurants, hotels, bars, over my 32 year career, and I did it by providing great service, being super dependable and being trustworthy. So, you can get anything you want in life if you help enough other people get what they want. The more you act like a salesperson, the less you're going to sell. People don't care how much you know, until they know how much you care. Oh, this is all sounds really great Ben, you're so slick and flowery in your language and how you speak. I'm telling you this is the truth. Put your focus on the restaurants, understand what they're up against. Service them, help them meet their goals and you'll sell more wine and spirits than you ever thought possible. You wanna own the wine list, you wanna own the buy the glass list, you wanna own the cocktail menu, the back bar, the banquet list, the room service list, the late night list, the happy hour list, you wanna own those things, then earn it. Earn it by paying attention to what's important to the restaurant. Lemme go back to that list of three things that every restaurant wants. Growing revenue, so how do you help a restaurant grow revenue? Well, you've gotta bring stuff that's going to sell and not collect dust. You've got to help him improve traffic. How will, how can you help a restaurant improve their foot traffic? How can you help them grow their check average? How can you increase incidents? And if you don't know what incidents is, then you got no business selling to a restaurant, go find out. And the second thing, controlling costs. Controlling costs just isn't always about lowering the price, it's about honoring the price you quoted. It's about being, you know, having some efficiencies in the operation, helping the operation lower inventory levels. You know, most restaurants have a fortune in inventory and turning inventory is not the most sexy part of buying wine but it's important. And you can help by bringing him stuff that's gonna turn and making sure that everything you've sold them is turning. So, controlling costs is a function of several things. Efficiency, you know, managing inventory and being, honoring the prices that you quote. The third thing which is improving guest satisfaction. Here's where it might come into play about your product. You know, is there a market for your product? Do you have a large social media following for your product? Do people have a passion for your product? Providing guest satisfaction in the restaurant is all about the experience, this quality value thing, delighting and surprising guests with new discoveries just of the wine category and the spirits category and the craft beer category is a wonderful place to do that. Great restaurant tourists curate, a selection of products that are gonna surprise and delight their guests. But that should be your approach. That should be your focus, not on what you need to sell. So, you wanna be successful selling wine to restaurants? I would replay this video, make a few notes and start to think about how important it is to not be just another salesperson, talking about your products, overcoming objections. You know, it's just, that isn't the way to sales success. So, if you wanna sell more wine, you wanna make big bonus checks, you wanna keep your boss off your back, you wanna win incentive trips, you wanna dominate your territory, these are the ways to do it. And until you figure it out, it will forever remain a secret.

- Whisky Cask Ownership Explained: Process, Passion & Investment | Sotheby's: Kind: captions Language: en What's the purpose of a cask in whiskey making? In short, it's transformation. Casks aren't just containers. They're catalysts. Over time, they shape a whisy's color, flavor, character. They add depth. They add complexity and nuance. Not just giving flavor to the whiskey, but also taking away unwanted characteristics. [Music] Scotch whiskey casts are always made of oak. And namely there are two key types. Most common is quirkus alba. This is American white oak. It's grown in North America and the USA. It imparts a golden character and flavors of coconut and vanilla. And then there's quus robo which is European oak and it gives a spicier character and often darker or redder color to the amber hues of the whiskey. In Scotland, casts are almost always reused or refilled. So they come to Scotland predominantly from either the bourbon or cherry industries and the history of where they've been matters. XBourbon barrels will impart sweetness, caramel, popcorn, vanilla, while cherry casts will impart richer flavors of Christmas spice and dried fruits. To legally be called whiskey, the spirit has to spend a minimum of 3 years in an oak cask, but often it's far longer. And the longer the spirit matures in its cask, the more it interacts with the wood and the wood seasoning. In Scotland, cast can be refilled and reused multiple times. So the fresher the barrel, the more color and the more flavor you put into that whiskey. The more the barrel is reused, the less impact it will have on the whiskey and its maturation. So what does it mean to own a cask? Unlike a bottle, cask ownership gives you access to the whiskey as it evolves. A whiskey's age statement denotes the amount of time it spends in a barrel. Once it's in a bottle, it ceases to age. Owners can choose when to bottle and whether to bottle at natural cast strength or lower, or whether to rerrack the cask and finish in a different wood type. Casks are assets that an owner can nurture, producing a whiskey that you can shape the journey of. At Sures, we work directly with cask owners from private collectors to distilleries to value, prepare, present these rare casts to the global market. We've been at the forefront of some of the most valuable whiskey cast sales in auction history. And now we're offering the opportunity for collectors to procure casks from us via private sales as well as auction. Whether you're selling one or starting a collection, we bring expertise and trust and reach. Every cast currently available at Sbees is one that I have either personally tasted or I visited in its warehouse. In every cast, there's not just whiskey, there's story, there's craft, and there's potential.

- How To Host A Wine Tasting Party? - Luxury Life Report: Kind: captions Language: en How to host a wine tasting party. Are you curious about how to host a wine tasting party that captures the essence of fine wine and its luxurious history? Let's dive into the steps that will make your gathering memorable and enjoyable. First, think about a theme for your wine tasting. You could focus on wines from a specific region like Bordeaux or Napa Valley or perhaps a grape variety such as Pinet Noir or Sovenon Blanc. A theme adds excitement and connects your event to the rich tradition of luxury gatherings. Next, assemble a selection of wines for a small group. In four five to seven bottles, each bottle typically serves five to six people with standard pores. If you prefer smaller pores, one bottle can serve up to 12 guests. Choose wines that are known for their craftsmanship and heritage as this reflects the luxury aspect of fine wine. Don't forget the glassware. Different wines benefit from specific glass shapes. For example, wide glasses enhance red wines while narrower ones suit whites. Using quality glassware not only improves the tasting experience but also shows your attention to detail. Set the ambience with thoughtful decor. Elegant table settings, soft lighting, and wine themed decorations can create a refined atmosphere. Consider adding background music and comfortable seating to make your guests feel special. Prepare pallet cleansing snacks and water. Light foods like bread or crackers help reset taste buds between wines. Water is essential for hydration and cleansing. For a luxurious touch, pair wines with premium cheeses or gourmet derves that complement their flavors. Provide tasting sheets or scorecards for your guests. This encourages them to jot down their impressions and reflected on the unique qualities of each wine. They can note aspects like aroma, flavor, and finish, which helps them appreciate the artistry behind fine wines. Think about incorporating food pairings. Select cheeses or small plates that highlight the characteristics of each wine. For instance, creamy bree pairs wonderfully with Chardonnay, while sharp cheddar compliments Cabernet Sovenon. Label each pairing and offer brief tasting notes to guide your guests. Make sure to have reliable tools on hand, like a quality corkcrew. Spitons can also be useful for guests who want to taste without consuming too much alcohol, allowing them to sample a wider variety. Encourage conversation among your guests. Wine tasting is about social connection as much as it is about the wine. Invite everyone to share their thoughts and preferences, creating a sense of community and appreciation for the finer things in life. Hosting a wine tasting party is a wonderful way to celebrate the craftsmanship and cultural significance of fine wines. By curating a selection of exceptional wines and providing an elegant setting, you create an event that honors the legacy of luxury and its impact on art and culture. This transforms a simple gathering into a sophisticated experience that reflects the values associated with luxury goods.

- Is Investing in Wine Better than the Stock Market?: Kind: captions Language: en Fine wines are one of the most sought after goods for those who enjoy the taste and class associated with them. With many wines, aging is one of the ways they continue to taste better and better. That means people are willing to pay more in the future for wines that age well. This has led to one of the most interesting forms of alternative investing, and even services have been created for collecting pricing data and coordinating the trading and exchange of investment wine. This is a Compounded Daily video. For more videos like this, make sure to subscribe to our channel. In March 2019, a wine auction at the world famous auction house, Christie’s was held for a variety of wines. During the auction, the most expensive sale was 12 bottles of wine from Burgundy, France for a little over $305,000. That is over $25,000 per bottle of wine. In 2011, the same case sold for between $97,000 and $116,000 in today’s dollars, marking an increase of over 126% over those 8 years. Overall, the auction seemed to be pretty successful with about 90% of all wines being sold. The rise in popularity of wines being bought from the secondary market has grown intensely from the year 2000 to about $4 billion in 2018. Although this is pretty insignificant if you compare it to total private equity investment activity of $800bn in 2018, $4Bn is still pretty significant. If you were to invest in wine, it’s not easy to pick the winner if you’re just buying any bottle of wine. Amongst the thousands of individual wineries out there, only around 250 produce wines that could be considered investment grade. Picking a winner involves knowing where to buy your wine from, and there are a few characteristics that you should know to look for if you want to pick a winning wine. The first is where the wine came from, more specifically, which region in France. Wines from vineyards in the region of Bordeaux account for 60% of all wine investment activity, and wines from Bergundy account for 20%. If you purchase a wine that’s not from either of these regions, then maybe you should consider just drinking it. Bordeux’s attractiveness to wine comes from its red clay soils that grow the grapes to produce its highly desired red wines. Additionally, the region takes its wine very seriously, designating very specific classifications to the elite among elite wines. In 1855, Napoleon Bonaparte III requested to make a classification system for France’s best Bordeaux wines to display to the rest of the world. They came up with Crus or “growths” ranked one to five for red wines. A Premiers Crus or “first growth”, for example, would signify the highest classification of wine. At the time, the law identified these wines based on the vineyard’s reputation and trading price. However, a lot of time has passed and many wineries have shrunk, yet there have only been two changes to the classification system. The first was in 1856 when Cantemerle was added as a fifth growth, and the second in 1973 when Chateu Mouton Rothschild was elevated from a second to first growth with the help of powerful lobbying by Philippe, Baron de Rothschild, who is, you guessed it, a member of the powerful Rothschild family. Another trait that should be examined when picking wine to invest in is, of course, the kind of wine. Fine champagnes age well for over 60 years, and port and fortified wines age well for over 100 years . Of course, buying a wine that is over 100 years old will be expensive, but buying it today before it is that price will be cheaper. If you were to buy a cheap white or red wine and hope that it would appreciate in price, much like wine that isnt from Bordeux or Borgundy, you should probably just drink it. Of course location and how well they age are not all that makes for a valuable wine. Having the approval of world renown wine critic Robert Parker would cause the price of wine to rise depending on his rating out of 100. His word would cause the price of wine to rise and fall, and when he gave a perfect 100 to one of the finest Bordeaux wines, the “Chateau Haut-Brion 1989”, its value increased tremendously. However, since he has stepped out of the picture in 2013, there have been no wine critics who have had this sort of effect. Now that we know what makes for a good investment wine, how does investing in these wines stack up against investing in the stock market? A good way to measure investment in the U.S. stock market is by looking at the S&P 500 index. Liv-Ex, a London based wine marketplace, created a variety of Indices to track the performance Its best performing index is the Liv-ex Fine Wine 1000 index, and it returned around 42% over the past 5 years. This is less than the S&P 500 over the same time period, which returned around 51%. However, within that index, the Burgundy 150 sub index returned over 93%, smashing the S&P 500. In a similar fashion, the top 1000 crus individually rose 264% over 15 years, beating the S&P 500 which grew 144%. So if you were able to pick the right 1000 wines, you would have been able to beat the market by a pretty good amount. Of course there are risks to investing in these fine wines. Because of their high value potential, there is a growing number of fraudulent wines that claim they are wines from the Bordeux region, or First Growth when they are not. If wines are something you want to invest into and hope to get a good return, you are going to want to store them somewhere safe and have insurance. In 2012, Hurricane Sandy flooded WineCare in New York City, destroying much of what the wines that were stored there. To much of a surprise, there was no insurance for the company. So are wines a good investment? Remember, most wines are not investment grade meaning that you should not go to the grocery store and stack up on wine. Also, expect to stay invested for more than 5 years if you hope to actually see a return on your investment. Unlike stock, you can’t just sell it whenever you need cash and hope to get your return that you were aiming for. Much like many alternative investments, finding a specific quality of wine might return greater than a stock. However, without Mr. Parker around, it may be difficult to determine what that next wine might be.
